<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1345489616">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1345489616" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/21081005-antonio" class="left imgcol" title="Antonio"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446948833p2/21081005.jpg" alt="Antonio" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1345489616?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/21081005-antonio" class="user" title="Antonio">Antonio</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/323461.Tiempos_Interesantes" title="Paperback 9788401335624" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1345489616">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer419989158258605615"><blockquote> <em>Hay una maldición.<br />Dice:<br />Ojalá vivas en tiempos interesantes.</em> </blockquote><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441866529i/16161952._SY540_.jpg" /><br />La quinta es la vencida, hasta ahora esta es la historia más sólida que leo del mundodisco sin dejar de ser graciosa, teniendo como protagonista a <strong>Rincewind</strong> el mago más cobarde que ha existido y que no importa que tanto trate de huir de todas las situaciones que se le presentan, los dioses o el destino, al parecer, siempre lo eligen como candidato para salvar al mundo. <br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441866529i/16161953._SX540_.jpg" /><br />Los personajes del libro me encantaron especialmente <strong> <em>la Horda de p</em></strong></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText419989158258605615"><blockquote>
  <em>Hay una maldición.<br />Dice:<br />Ojalá vivas en tiempos interesantes.</em>
</blockquote><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441866529i/16161952._SY540_.jpg" /><br />La quinta es la vencida, hasta ahora esta es la historia más sólida que leo del mundodisco sin dejar de ser graciosa, teniendo como protagonista a <strong>Rincewind</strong> el mago más cobarde que ha existido y que no importa que tanto trate de huir de todas las situaciones que se le presentan, los dioses o el destino, al parecer, siempre lo eligen como candidato para salvar al mundo. <br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441866529i/16161953._SX540_.jpg" /><br />Los personajes del libro me encantaron especialmente <strong>
  <em>la Horda de plata</em>
</strong>, que prueba que la edad es solo un número (que probablemente ellos no sepan contar) y que para ser héroe solo importa la actitud y la barbarie, a esta cuerda de viejitos cool se les une el <strong>Señor Saveloy “el profe”</strong> que tratara <s>sin mucho éxito</s> de civilizarlos.<br /><blockquote>
  <em>¿Sabe? Resulta usted muy culto para ser un bárbaro —dijo Rincewind.<br /> —Oh, cielos, yo no soy bárbaro de siempre. Yo era maestro de escuela. Por eso me llaman Profe.<br /> —¿Y qué enseñaba?<br />—Geografía. Y me interesaban mucho los estudios aurientales. Pero decidí dejarlo para ganarme la vida con la espada.<br />—¿Después de ser profesor toda su vida?<br />—Supuso un cambio de perspectiva, sí.<br />—Pero... bueno., seguramente... las privaciones... los peligros terribles, el riesgo diario de muerte…<br />El señor Saveloy se animó de pronto:<br />—Ah, también usted ha sido maestro, ¿verdad?</em>
</blockquote><br />De una forma satírica y atrayente Pratchett describe la cultura China, y lo cruel que puede ser una rebelión (muy al estilo Rebelión en la granja), por ello, y porque el final me encanto, este es, hasta ahora mi libro favorito del mundodisco, por eso pienso leer más de sus historias descabelladas.<br /><blockquote>
  <em>—No —dijo Rincewind.<br /> —Pero no se puede hundir uno en el hielo.<br />—No. Uno se resbala primero y luego se hunde y luego el hielo le golpea en la cabeza. Sin contar a las ballenas asesinas. Y unas focas enormes com lof diemtef afí.<br />—Esto es descabellado, lo sé —dijo el tesorero en tono jovial.<br /> —¿El qué? —preguntó el conferenciante de Runas Recientes.<br /> —Esta parte de la cabeza donde se me está cayendo el pelo.</em>
</blockquote><br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_195202509">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/195202509" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5973959-zach" class="left imgcol" title="Zach"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1312995384p2/5973959.jpg" alt="Zach" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/195202509?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 16, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5973959-zach" class="user" title="Zach">Zach</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5973959-zach?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer195202509">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5384037025612052080">I'm going to just say right off the bat that the Rincewind novels are not my favorite. I realize that it is Discworld blasphemy, but hear me out. Out of the entire Discworld main character cast, Rincewind makes the fewest decisions and has no desires other than to flee from trouble. As sort of a Forrest Gump/Scooby Doo hybrid (Forrest Doo? Scooby Gump?), his misadventures consist of finding himself in trouble, trying to escape, and then lucking out in some grand fashion. While it makes for good</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5384037025612052080">I'm going to just say right off the bat that the Rincewind novels are not my favorite. I realize that it is Discworld blasphemy, but hear me out. Out of the entire Discworld main character cast, Rincewind makes the fewest decisions and has no desires other than to flee from trouble. As sort of a Forrest Gump/Scooby Doo hybrid (Forrest Doo? Scooby Gump?), his misadventures consist of finding himself in trouble, trying to escape, and then lucking out in some grand fashion. While it makes for good entertainment, I respond better to characters who actually try to tackle problems, whether it be through scheming (Vetinari), trickery (the witches), or just good old-fashioned detective work (the City Watch).<br /><br />That being said, the highlights:<br /><br />-- The dialogue. Once again, Pratchett employs rapid fire exchanges to great effect, and his characterization is so solid that formally acknowledging who said what is not necessary. You can see this primarily in the dialogue between the Unseen University faculty and also the Silver Horde (Whut?).<br /><br />-- The jokes. As is typical, there is no shortage of puns, jokes, and cultural references here, from the multiple meanings of Auriental words to several different running gags [urinating dog].<br /><br />-- Ankh Morpork vs. the Agatean Empire. While not necessarily laugh-out-loud funny, there are several passages that draw astute comparisons between life in Ankh Morpork and the Empire, ranging from peasant life vs. the ruling elite to the concept of fiat currency. These illustrate that Discworld is more than just trite satire--it provides a commentary on our own history and society.<br /><br />Also of note -- It's very strange that I've read two consecutive books that make a reference to the Naglfar, a boat made from the fingernails and toenails of the dead, from Norse mythology.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5384037025612052080">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1364387501">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1364387501" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies" class="left imgcol" title="David Sarkies"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445680853p2/5856247.jpg" alt="David Sarkies" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1364387501?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 22, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies" class="user" title="David Sarkies">David Sarkies</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/386368.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780552142359" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who love Pratchett (and those who don't)</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      Stewart Wymer
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy" class="actionLinkLite">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1364387501">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14352501917813951391"><strong>Rincewind in China</strong><br />22 August 2015<br /><br /> When my friend leant me this book he simply said 'Rincewind' at which I rolled my eyes. I must say that Rincewind is certainly not my favourite Discworld character, even though some of the books in which he has starred have been quite good. However I wasn't really expecting anything all that much to come from this book, even though it is one of the Discworld novels and I am slowly making my way through each of them (and it was also a bit of a time out from some o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14352501917813951391"><strong>Rincewind in China</strong><br />22 August 2015<br /><br />	When my friend leant me this book he simply said 'Rincewind' at which I rolled my eyes. I must say that Rincewind is certainly not my favourite Discworld character, even though some of the books in which he has starred have been quite good. However I wasn't really expecting anything all that much to come from this book, even though it is one of the Discworld novels and I am slowly making my way through each of them (and it was also a bit of a time out from some of the heavier novels that I have read).<br /><br />	It turned out that it was a good thing that I didn't expect all that much from this book because if you aren't expecting all that much then it is really easy to exceed those expectations. In fact I really enjoyed this book, and even Rincewind was quite cool, especially when you appreciate the nature of his character (namely that his main rule of survival is to run away as fast as possible, and that a boring life is a good life because a boring life means that your lifespan is inevitably going to be much longer).<br /><br />	This time Pratchett takes us to China, or at least Discworld's equivalent of China – the Agatean Empire. This was a little odd because with the first book the suggestion was that the Agatean Empire was in fact Japan (in which Pratchett was poking fun at the stereotypical Japanese Tourist – Twoflower). However, it now turns out that it is China and that people weren't supposed to actually leave the country and visit the world on the other side of the wall. In fact the powers that be discourage that simply by telling everybody that there is nothing behind the wall except demons and ghosts.<br /><br />	Thus therein lies the problem. Not only did Twoflower leave but when he returned he wrote a book called 'What I did on my Holidays'. Sure, it sound's like some primary school essay, but I am sure that primary school essays have managed to morph themselves into revolutionary documents. Actually, there have been countless numbers of books that have morphed themselves into revolutionary documents, despite the author having no intention beyond simply writing a fascinating story.<br /><br />	The other issue is that the emperor is dying, which means that there is an opening for a new emperor. Sure, the Emperor should have an heir, but that doesn't seem to be the case here, and even if he did having an heir does not necessarily mean that that heir is going to become emperor, namely because there is some other guy who wants that title – Lord Hong. As one can expect from a farcical comedy, nothing seems to go right for poor Hong. Sure, he manages to kill the emperor, but before he can take the title as his own he suddenly discovers that somebody has beaten him to the punch – Cohen the Barbarian, or as we find out – Genghiz Cohen.<br /><br /><img width="320" height="222" class="escapedImg" alt="Cohen the Barbarian" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440226833i/15949391._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />	If there is one thing that I absolutely loved about this book it has to be the Silver Horde (which is a play on the Golden Horde, otherwise known as the Mongals). Here we have a handful of old men, who happen to be barbarians, sneaking into the city to steal the throne of the empire. Sure, most people expect old men to be weak and frail, but that would be a huge mistake when approaching the Silver Horde. In fact at one point the end up beating up a room full of ninja.<br /><br />	I also quite liked the idea of the wall. We all think that the Great Wall of China was built to keep the Mongal Hordes out (and good job it did to – NOT), however Pratchett suggests that that is not the case – it is designed to keep the people inside. We can't have plebs leaving the country, learning new ideas, such as rebellion, and then bringing it back and corrupting people – they've already had a problem with one person doing that. Anyway, as Cohen says, how is a wall going to stop a horde of barbarians? They ain't going to get there, see the wall, and decide that it is all too hard and go home again. No, they are going to take wood from all the trees lying around and then build ladders so they can climb over it.<br /><br />	This whole thing about keeping the people in made me think about China back when it was written. Back then you hardly had any Chinese travelling abroad – these days you have heaps. I was sort of wandering whether Pratchett was having a dig at some of these closed countries, though prior to the 19th Century China was a pretty closed country and many of the peasants were intentionally kept ignorant so as not to upset the balance (apparently the Chinese Script was invented to make it really hard for the general populace to become literate). Still, I'm not sure if you are meant to think too deeply with a Terry Pratchett book (though then again it is satire, so I am probably wrong), but then again it is something that I do enjoy doing.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14352501917813951391">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_452820120">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/452820120" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1343864-ashley" class="left imgcol" title="Ashley"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1254118403p2/1343864.jpg" alt="Ashley" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/452820120?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 11, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1343864-ashley" class="user" title="Ashley">Ashley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=satire" class="actionLinkLite">satire</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=treasure-and-adventure" class="actionLinkLite">treasure-and-adventure</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=anglophilia" class="actionLinkLite">anglophilia</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=swashbucklers-capers-and-heists" class="actionLinkLite">swashbucklers-capers-and-heists</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=ridiculously-prolific-authors" class="actionLinkLite">ridiculously-prolific-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer452820120">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9274001516004813663">One of the worst curses you can fling at a Discworld character is “May you live in interesting times,” hence the title of the book.<br /><br />But aside from it’s promising title, the 17th Discworld book was a bit of a letdown after the fitting soulful musings of <em>Soul Music</em>, but it almost made up for it in sheer volume of jokes and witticisms alone. I wanted to quote something practically every other page. The perfect Discworld book is funny, biting, and deep-hitting. This one was mostly just amusing, altho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9274001516004813663">One of the worst curses you can fling at a Discworld character is “May you live in interesting times,” hence the title of the book.<br /><br />But aside from it’s promising title, the 17th Discworld book was a bit of a letdown after the fitting soulful musings of <em>Soul Music</em>, but it almost made up for it in sheer volume of jokes and witticisms alone. I wanted to quote something practically every other page. The perfect Discworld book is funny, biting, and deep-hitting. This one was mostly just amusing, although it did have some in-world continuity to fall back on that gave it a little extra oomph.<br /><br />Although it can be read on its own, <em>Interesting Times</em> is a Rincewind book, and in many ways it’s actually a direct sequel to the very first Discworld books, <em>The Colour of Magic</em> and <em>The Light Fantastic</em>. We first met Rincewind and the flat disc of a world he lives on (carried around space by four giant elephants perched on the back of an even more giant turtle) when he is shanghaied into playing guide to a hapless tourist named Twoflower from the faraway Counterweight Continent. The two of them have many ridiculous adventures together, which mostly consist of Twoflower being delighted by everything, including and most especially when his life is in danger, and Rincewind being inept and terrified by it all, but somehow stumbling through and saving the day anyway. <em>Interesting Times</em> turns the tables and finds Rincewind an unexpected tourist on the Counterweight Continent, only it seems he’s been called there because Revolution is brewing, and Twoflower’s tales of his adventures in Ankh-Morpork and beyond (which he wrote up and titled “What I Did on My Summer Holidays”) have lent him the moniker The Great Wizard. The people want him to help them overthrow their cruel Empire, a task for which he is monumentally unfit.<br /><br />There’s also some stuff with The Luggage and a group of old, old men (like, SERIOUSLY old) led by Cohen the Barbarian who are also in the country to do nefarious things to the Empire. The joke with them is mostly that they’re so old you’d think they’d be incompetent but they are still the most deadly people in the room, even the one in the wheel chair, and there’s this whole thing about them trying to learn to be “civilized.” It mostly all works.<br /><br />The ending felt a little too coincidental for me, and again, it was mostly all surface level humor (although still very funny), so it’s definitely not one of my favorites in this series, but still a good read. What this book lacks in plotting and such it makes up for in enthusiasm and jokes about really old mangy men. <br /><br />[3.5 stars]</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9274001516004813663">...more</a>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer645149573">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16298231169348864176">Hmm. Well. My husband read this one immediately before I did and said, handing me the book, "I'm interested to know whether you think this is racist, or somehow parodying stereotypes of Asian people?" Having finished it, I too feel uncertain.<br /><br />This is a really funny book. It's Pratchett, after all, and it's got Ridcully and Ponder Stibbons and Rincewind's talent for surviving (aka running away while screaming ohshitohshitohshit I'm going to die) and Conan and some other extremely elderly barbarian</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16298231169348864176">Hmm. Well. My husband read this one immediately before I did and said, handing me the book, "I'm interested to know whether you think this is racist, or somehow parodying stereotypes of Asian people?" Having finished it, I too feel uncertain.<br /><br />This is a really funny book. It's Pratchett, after all, and it's got Ridcully and Ponder Stibbons and Rincewind's talent for surviving (aka running away while screaming ohshitohshitohshit I'm going to die) and Conan and some other extremely elderly barbarian heroes, and that is all for the good. The existence of a sort of Chinese replica of C.M.O.T. Dibbler is pleasing, certainly. And as far as I'm concerned the book is made by Ridcully's absolute inability to accept that opposite points on a disc are traveling in different directions (both are going round and round! he insists--and his grasp of gravity's logistics is delightfully shaky, too). And yet the humor around Chinese-people-are-sooooo-different-they-have-funny-names falls rather flat for me.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_14146551">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14146551" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/858949-chris" class="left imgcol" title="Chris"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1208350750p2/858949.jpg" alt="Chris" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14146551?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/858949-chris" class="user" title="Chris">Chris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/386368.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780552142359" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=pratchett" class="actionLinkLite">pratchett</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14146551">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13985379249623647114">There is a saying, often attributed to the Chinese - "May you live in interesting times." Usually when this is invoked, it's done so as a curse, the idea being that interesting times are more likely to cause you trouble than nice boring times, and perhaps that's true. The folks in Baghdad, for example, are certainly living in interesting times right now. The trouble is that not everybody is able to stay alive to enjoy them.<br /><br />That's one of the problems with life as we know it - we long for things t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13985379249623647114">There is a saying, often attributed to the Chinese - "May you live in interesting times." Usually when this is invoked, it's done so as a curse, the idea being that interesting times are more likely to cause you trouble than nice boring times, and perhaps that's true. The folks in Baghdad, for example, are certainly living in interesting times right now. The trouble is that not everybody is able to stay alive to enjoy them.<br /><br />That's one of the problems with life as we know it - we long for things to be interesting, exciting and thrilling, like what happens to Bruce Willis every time he's on the screen. When those times come, however, we realize that it's the boring, predictable times we really want. In other words, we want whatever we <em>don't</em> have at the moment, which just goes to prove that we, as a species, are messed up in the head. If we had been assembled by any rational Supreme Being, it would have made us a little more accepting of the lives we lead. This mind-set may not lead us to the advanced society we have now, but it certainly would lead us to something approaching world peace.<br /><br />This book is about wanting what you don't have, and what happens when you get it.<br /><br />The central character is the wizard - or Wizzard - Rincewind, one of the oldest of the Discworld characters. He's been with the series since the first book, <em>The Colour of Magic</em>, and he's grown to be a favorite for many readers. What Rincewind wants, <em>really</em> wants, is to be left alone. No quests, no challenges, no one trying to kill him or otherwise ruin his day. If the world forgot that Rincewind existed, he'd be the happiest man alive.<br /><br />Unfortunately for Rincewind, the world hasn't forgotten him. He has to be sent to the far-off Agatean Empire, a place so remote that few, if any, people know anything about it. A message came, asking for the Great Wizzard, and Rincewind is the only one who fits the bill. The fact that he can't do magic is not important, really.<br /><br />When he gets there, he meets Cohen the Barbarian and his Silver Horde - seven incredibly old barbarian heroes. Seven men who don't know the meaning of the word "defeat," though you'd probably have to repeat it very loudly before they heard what you'd said. Together, the Horde are headed to the capital city of the Empire, looking to make the biggest heist in their long, long, long barbarian careers.<br /><br />Together, Rincewind, Cohen and the Horde find the Empire in the throes of a people's revolution, borne of righteous peasant rage and the skillful manipulations of the Grand Vizier, Lord Hong.<br /><br />Like so many Discworld books, this is a lot of fun to read. The Agatean Empire is a blend of ancient China and Japan, giving us ninja and samurai alongside blue and white Ming ceramics and an exam-based bureaucracy. And like most of the other Discworld books, this one gives you something to think about - what do you want to be?<br /><br />Rincewind wants to be left alone, because he thinks he'll be safer that way. Cohen wants to settle down, because he worries that his life as a barbarian hero might be catching up to him. Lord Hong wants to be a gentleman of Ankh-Morpork, or at least the ruler of such men. And the people of the Empire, who call themselves the Red Army, want to be free, even though they have no idea what being free means.<br /><br />The only character who seems to change his life for the better is Mister Saveloy, the youngest member of the Silver Horde and the one they call "Teach." He realized that what he thought he wanted - a life of educating young people - wasn't what he really wanted after all. What he wanted was the certainty and simplicity of Cohen's barbarian lifestyle, and found it rather agreed with him.<br /><br />So what's the lesson here? Perhaps this: Be happy with what you have, but don't be afraid to change. Just remember that not all change is for the better.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13985379249623647114">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer122925519">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer703074082754453693">I rarely think of rereading this particular discworld book - not for any reason I can guess. My kids started reading this one aloud and sucked me right into it, and I am very glad. It is a truely delightful book, although we all tend to moan a bit about the awful things that Terry Pratchett came up with to keep doing to Rincewind.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1423386640">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6748136114528505399">While Death only has a very small bit in this book, I still loved this one. Fate playing games with the others at the beginning is a great bit and there is lots to love in Cohen the Barbarian. <br /><br />I am saddened that after having finished this one, I only have fourteen Discworld books that I have not read now. <br /><br />Four stars.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_168812872">

  <a href="/user/show/5454475-tfitoby" class="left imgcol" title="Tfitoby"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1342170152p2/5454475.jpg" alt="Tfitoby" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/168812872?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 09, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5454475-tfitoby" class="user" title="Tfitoby">Tfitoby</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/386368.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780552142359" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5454475-tfitoby?shelf=fantastical" class="actionLinkLite">fantastical</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer168812872">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3439832648094909037">Essentially this is silly, racist and obvious, a typical Rincewind book really. But to counter that you have a lot of fun with Cohen the Barbarian and to counter that you have and old man in a wheelchair whose sole purpose for being in the novel is to shout "What?!" to everything. Because he's deaf you see. Yeah it's that kind of Discworld book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/5743748-wastrel" class="user" title="Wastrel">Wastrel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/386368.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780552142359" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=r-ps" class="actionLinkLite">r-ps</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=z-1995" class="actionLinkLite">z-1995</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=fantastika-social-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantastika-social-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer804940420">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17464968689794382769">Short version? Pratchett tries to return to his earlier style with the lessons he's learnt along the way. This starts out promisingly. Unfortunately then he tries to combine that earlier, sillier style with multiple serious political discussions and several unrelated books, all in a setting that is, to put it mildly, culturally insensitive. It doesn't work.<br /><br />There are some great lines, but by and large it's just too dull and despite its good qualities it outstays its welcome (50 pages of long, dra</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17464968689794382769">Short version? Pratchett tries to return to his earlier style with the lessons he's learnt along the way. This starts out promisingly. Unfortunately then he tries to combine that earlier, sillier style with multiple serious political discussions and several unrelated books, all in a setting that is, to put it mildly, culturally insensitive. It doesn't work.<br /><br />There are some great lines, but by and large it's just too dull and despite its good qualities it outstays its welcome (50 pages of long, drawn-out ending doesn't help either).<br /><br />In my opinion the first real misfire of the series (distinct from the flaws of the crawling-on-baby-legs early books) Probably one for completists. That said, I can see what he's trying to do. And let's be honest, a bad Pratchett novel is still an OK book that would make entertaining enough light reading. It's not a bad book. It's just not up to the standards of the series.<br /><br /><br />My full (and much, much more substantial) review <a rel="nofollow" href="http://vacuouswastrel.wordpress.com/2014/09/24/interesting-times-by-terry-pratchett/">is on my blog over here.</a></span>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer901168929">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6203243647233975196">Yay for seeing Twoflower again! And any story with Cohen tends to be funny. I wish Lord Hong had been less one-dimensional though: his whole character revolved around "I can do everything better than everyone else." That gets boring very quickly. I would have loved to see him interact with Vetinari, since he held him in such high regard.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText158788837923275643">Un libro genial, muy divertido y con algunas caras conocidas que, dicho sea de paso, ya extrañaba.<br /><br />Lo que mas me a gustado de este libro a sido La Horda, esa pandilla de ancianos que vienen de tiempos mas brutales y que no saben como comportarse civilizadamente.<br /><br />Si bien todo el libro es genial me han gustado mas las partes en que La Horda hace acto de presencia, se nota su nostalgia por tiempos mas simples, cada frase que pronuncian esta cargada de recuerdos de tiempos mas barbaros y mejores, y para un fan de Conan el Barbaro como lo soy yo el sentimiento es compartido al 100 por ciento. <br /><br />... ¡ah! y también hay una revolución.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_806253021">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/806253021" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9698647-matt" class="left imgcol" title="Matt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1341102729p2/9698647.jpg" alt="Matt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/806253021?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 14, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9698647-matt" class="user" title="Matt">Matt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer806253021">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10204237195933308283">Discworld’s Counterweight Continent is explored for the first time in the series as author Terry Pratchett sends the inept wizard Rincewind to the walled off landmass where he meets up with some old friends in a reunion of the series’ first two books.<br /><br />The Discworld’s version of China &amp; Japan is the Agatean Empire, a mysterious place which only the rest of the Discworld can speculate about, sends a message to Ankh-Morpork for the ‘Great Wizzard’. After several uses of magical quantum mechanics</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10204237195933308283">Discworld’s Counterweight Continent is explored for the first time in the series as author Terry Pratchett sends the inept wizard Rincewind to the walled off landmass where he meets up with some old friends in a reunion of the series’ first two books.<br /><br />The Discworld’s version of China &amp; Japan is the Agatean Empire, a mysterious place which only the rest of the Discworld can speculate about, sends a message to Ankh-Morpork for the ‘Great Wizzard’. After several uses of magical quantum mechanics transportation and threats Rincewind finds himself in the middle of a battlefield as the five warlord families are preparing for the succession war upon the Emperor’s death. Unfortunately for Rincewind he finds himself the focus of the rebellious Red Army as well as Lord Hong, who is secretly funding the rebels as part of his plan to conquer Ankh-Morpork once he is Emperor. Along with Rincewind return to prominence is Cohen the Barbarian and Twoflower, though the former’s story arc is bigger and best secondary plot of the book while the latter’s two daughters are part of the Red Army’s leadership. And despite his best efforts Rincewind is always in the center of the action as he is unknowingly the favorite ‘pawn’ of The Lady in her game against Fate.<br /><br />The return of Rincewind and Cohen after so long being written about is a welcoming development in ‘Interesting Times’ and Pratchett seems to enjoy allowing his first protagonist to suffer the excitement of grand adventures, especially after seeing where Rincewind finds himself at the end of the book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10204237195933308283">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_835055453">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/835055453" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/23437938-richard-kirby" class="left imgcol" title="Richard Kirby"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Richard Kirby" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/835055453?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 26, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/23437938-richard-kirby" class="user" title="Richard Kirby">Richard Kirby</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer835055453">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11646902640997863792">The seventeenth novel in Pratchett’s Discworld series, the title of this novel, referring to the Chinese curse ‘May you live in interesting times’, alludes to the setting as the fictional Agatean Empire, an amalgam of different aspects of Chinese and Japanese culture and history. The hapless hero Rincewind is sent there following a request from the Agatean Grand Vizier, the villainous Lord Hong, who aspires to be Emperor.<br />More by accident than design, Rincewind foils Lord Hong and helps his frien</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11646902640997863792">The seventeenth novel in Pratchett’s Discworld series, the title of this novel, referring to the Chinese curse ‘May you live in interesting times’, alludes to the setting as the fictional Agatean Empire, an amalgam of different aspects of Chinese and Japanese culture and history. The hapless hero Rincewind is sent there following a request from the Agatean Grand Vizier, the villainous Lord Hong, who aspires to be Emperor.<br />More by accident than design, Rincewind foils Lord Hong and helps his friend, the ancient barbarian, Genghiz Cohen, a play on the name of the real Genghiz Khan, become Emperor instead.<br /><br />Loaded with references to China and Japan, such as terracotta warriors, sumo wrestling and origami, this would make a challenging but enjoyable story for high ability KS2 readers. The references to this increasingly dynamic part of the world could be used as launch pads for studies of East Asian culture and history, while the book itself, filled with Pratchett’s enjoyable and very funny send up of fantasy novels like the Lord of the Rings, could not but help encourage any reader to explore the rest of the Discworld series of books.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11646902640997863792">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6970358614727642580">"Discworld's Greatest Hits" might have been an alternate title for this book. Long-suffering Rincewind is pulled back in for the amusement of the gods (and the reader), and has the usual Rincewind-ish adventures: that is to say, fleeing danger whenever possible, and landing in it just as often. Surely fan service for the original two novels, secondary characters are broadly drawn but thinly developed, falling into one of Pratchett's favorite formulas of <em>Terrible Villain Receives His Comeuppance.</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6970358614727642580">"Discworld's Greatest Hits" might have been an alternate title for this book. Surely fan service for the original two novels, secondary characters are broadly drawn but thinly developed, falling into one of Pratchett's favorite formulas of <em>Terrible Villain Receives His Comeuppance.</em> A light read that does not aim for any sort of real target to satirize. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1000751784">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1000751784" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9098678-scott-holstad" class="left imgcol" title="Scott Holstad"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1335536381p2/9098678.jpg" alt="Scott Holstad" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1000751784?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 16, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9098678-scott-holstad" class="user" title="Scott Holstad">Scott Holstad</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1000751784">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17209851715887859066">I finished this Discworld novel so many weeks ago that I can't remember if I was going to give it four stars or five! I think five, so that's what I'll do. I also can't remember enough to give it a decent review, and for that, I truly apologize. I've read too many books in between. <br /><br />This book is another in the Rincewind the Wizzard series, and it's really very good. In it, he's chosen to go to the Agatean Empire on the mysterious Counterweight Continent -- a place that appears to be like ancient</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17209851715887859066">I finished this Discworld novel so many weeks ago that I can't remember if I was going to give it four stars or five! I think five, so that's what I'll do. I also can't remember enough to give it a decent review, and for that, I truly apologize. I've read too many books in between. <br /><br />This book is another in the Rincewind the Wizzard series, and it's really very good. In it, he's chosen to go to the Agatean Empire on the mysterious Counterweight Continent -- a place that appears to be like ancient China, with a little Japanese thrown in for good measure. The emperor is dying and various factions are vying to take over upon his death. However, there's an ancient legend that at just such a time in centuries past, a great wizard rose up with his Red Army and won the war, enabling the "right" emperor to live on. Rincewind is supposed to be that wizard, reincarnated. Of course they don't know he's inept. But he's got his magical Luggage with him. And he runs into Twoflower again! Not only that, but he runs into Cohen the Barbarian, who with several other ancient barbarian heroes, have decided to ruthlessly take over the Agatean Empire and rule it themselves. It's seven and against an army of 700,000. They like their odds. The rebels Rincewind encounters are exceedingly polite. Their slogans are hilarious. And of course, as you knew would have to happen, Rincewind saves the day. I won't tell you how, though. You have to read it to find that out.<br /><br />Interesting Times is an interesting book and Pratchett does a nice job of taking non-pc, yet playful, jabs at other cultures, while still taking a few at his own. I don't know if this is the last Rincewind book I'll read -- I suspect it is -- but the character couldn't have gone out on a higher note. Certainly recommended.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17209851715887859066">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/984198365?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 02, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/23051470-rachel" class="user" title="Rachel">Rachel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/386368.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780552142359" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/23051470-rachel?shelf=fantasy-adult" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/23051470-rachel?shelf=the-discworld-series" class="actionLinkLite">the-discworld-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer984198365">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6011326767802698431">Once again Rincewind finds himself a pawn in the game of the gods. Even on the Counterweight Continent trouble befalls him, for no matter where he goes the people are the same, just in a different setting. In this highly structured society, the people are oppressed into obedience and politeness. Some have had enough, but how do you start a revolution in a country that doesn't know how to rise up? Rincewind's original adventure comes back to haunt him, when Twoflower's book on his "holiday" becom</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6011326767802698431">Once again Rincewind finds himself a pawn in the game of the gods. Even on the Counterweight Continent trouble befalls him, for no matter where he goes the people are the same, just in a different setting. In this highly structured society, the people are oppressed into obedience and politeness. Some have had enough, but how do you start a revolution in a country that doesn't know how to rise up? Rincewind's original adventure comes back to haunt him, when Twoflower's book on his "holiday" becomes the manual for the Red Army. Due to votes, negotiations, commands, threats, and a heaping pile of pure coincidence, Rincewind gets pushed ever deeper into the role of the "Great Wizzard"....no matter how fast he runs away. <br /><br />Aside from Twoflower, we also see the return of the Luggage, and Cohen, who struggles with the concepts of a "civilized" people. Just like the rest of the Discworld, the Agatean Empire is based off a real-world culture, in this case Asian ethnicity, with a particular emphasis on its written form,and government. <br /><br />Pratchett's quirky dialogue works perfectly in the context of politics, which tend to obfuscate the truth. Rincewind himself is quite amusing; his cowardice means he always overreacts, however this is most striking because his assessment of the situation is always spot on. Luck is on his side, but he always bares the brunt of unlucky events. Between the match of Fate and Luck, and Butterflies, he truly lives in interesting times.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6011326767802698431">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_901214567">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/901214567" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1235896-doreen" class="left imgcol" title="Doreen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1348630486p2/1235896.jpg" alt="Doreen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/901214567?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 06, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1235896-doreen" class="user" title="Doreen">Doreen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer901214567">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10822716260117452567">I love all the Terry Pratchett I've read so far, so I really wanted this to be better than it was. I've heard that the Rincewind books are the weakest of the Discworld novels, and the first few pages were certainly an oddly paced morass of characters coming towards a cohesive plot, but my real problem with the book was the way it excused casual racism, especially in Cohen the Barbarian (who was a pretty awesome character otherwise.) Contrary to Pratchett's assertion, charisma is no excuse for bi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10822716260117452567">I love all the Terry Pratchett I've read so far, so I really wanted this to be better than it was. I've heard that the Rincewind books are the weakest of the Discworld novels, and the first few pages were certainly an oddly paced morass of characters coming towards a cohesive plot, but my real problem with the book was the way it excused casual racism, especially in Cohen the Barbarian (who was a pretty awesome character otherwise.) Contrary to Pratchett's assertion, charisma is no excuse for bigotry. I was particularly annoyed by the reasoning, at one point, of how one can't possibly be a racist when faced with death at the hands of an ethnic group. I want to give Pratchett the benefit of the doubt by thinking that that passage was meant to be satirical, but I'm usually pretty good at figuring out that sort of thing, and frankly did not find it amusing (I know, I know, the war cry of killjoys everywhere.)<br /><br />It's just... I've always found the word "idealistic" to be something of a pejorative, but even I was uncomfortable with the way the revolutionaries were portrayed as either naive or proto-fascist. I thought the way Pratchett presented the interests of the literate in preserving the interests of the illiterate ham-handedly unfair, even if I agree with his underlying thesis of the good of education. And though I'm not an Asian of Far Eastern descent, I was also uncomfortable with the weirdly racist undertones to certain observations in the text. Tbh, I might not even have been bothered by said undertones if the overt mentions of racism hadn't been so oddly defensive. I'm not the kind of person who thinks that a stereotype is automatically racist, but the inclusion of too many stereotypes would trend the reader towards that verdict.<br /><br />Otherwise, I enjoyed the humor and inventiveness characteristic of the Discworld series. I'm just glad this wasn't my introduction to it, though I do remember not being able to get past the first few pages of the first Rincewind book a long, long time ago (forced myself to read Burgess' A Clockwork Orange instead, which should give you an idea of comparative readability there.) Loved Teach, especially, and his bittersweet fate.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10822716260117452567">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_295375293">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/295375293" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1412589-remo" class="left imgcol" title="Remo"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1330850679p2/1412589.jpg" alt="Remo" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/295375293?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 16, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1412589-remo" class="user" title="Remo">Remo</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/323461.Tiempos_Interesantes" title="Paperback 9788401335624" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1412589-remo?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1412589-remo?shelf=novela" class="actionLinkLite">novela</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1412589-remo?shelf=2006" class="actionLinkLite">2006</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer295375293">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10235939186856958598"><p>Nueva entrega de las aventuras del Mundodisco, con Rincewind como protagonista. En esta ocasión, Terry Pratchett [TP] manda a Rincewind a China (bueno, al equivalente de China en el Mundodisco, situada en el Continente Contrapeso). Rincewind deberá ayudar a que tenga lugar una Revolución muy educada, al tiempo que se hace amigo de la Horda, formada por Genghis Cohen y seis ancianos más. Al mismo tiempo, todo se ve trastocado por la Mariposa Del Clima. Sí, hombre, esa mariposa que bate las alas e</p></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10235939186856958598"><p>Nueva entrega de las aventuras del Mundodisco, con Rincewind como protagonista. En esta ocasión, Terry Pratchett [TP] manda a Rincewind a China (bueno, al equivalente de China en el Mundodisco, situada en el Continente Contrapeso). Rincewind deberá ayudar a que tenga lugar una Revolución muy educada, al tiempo que se hace amigo de la Horda, formada por Genghis Cohen y seis ancianos más. Al mismo tiempo, todo se ve trastocado por la Mariposa Del Clima™. Sí, hombre, esa mariposa que bate las alas en Brasil y provoca un tornado en Pekín (alguien debería echarle insecticida a la mariposilla de los demonios). Leamos la introducción que nos hace TP de este curioso animalito:<br /></p><blockquote>
<br />La Mariposa Cuántica del Clima (<em>Papilio tempestae</em>) es de un color amarillo corriente, aunque los fractales de Mandelbrot que tiene dibujados en sus alas presentan un interés considerable. Su rasgo más destacado es la capacidad de producir fenómenos climáticos.<br /><p>Esto empezó probablemente como un rasgo destinado a la supervivencia, ya que hasta el pájaro más hambriento se echaría atrás ante un tornado lanzado a mala fe. Más adelante es posible que se convirtiera en un rasgo sexual secundario, como el plumaje de los pájaros o los sacos vocales de ciertas ranas. <em>Mírame</em>, dice el macho batiendo perezosamente las alas bajo el dosel de la selva. Puede que sea de un amarillo corriente, pero en cosa de dos semanas, a dos mil kilómetros de distancia, “Violentos vendavales provocan un caos circulatorio”. <br />Ésta es la mariposa de las tormentas. Bate las alas…</p>
</blockquote><br /><br />La novela es desternillante. Aparecen los Guerreros de Xian, la Ciudad Prohibida, el Emperador, los mercados chinos, y TP hace chistes de todos ellos. Una de las escenas finales, épica, nos muestra a siete ancianos de la Horda listos para enfrentarse a setecientos mil guerreros de los Cinco Señores de la Guerra, ofreciéndoles la rendición (los ancianos a los Señores) para que nadie resulte herido. Está muy bien, me la he leído casi de un tirón y con una sonrisa puesta todo el rato. La novela lleva el número 18 de la serie en castellano, pero es anterior a la número 9, “El país del fin del mundo”. En ésta, Rincewind termina en Australia, que es justo donde empieza aquélla. En resumen, que me lo he pasado pipa con esta nueva entrega. <br />Mi nota: <em>Divertidísima</em>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10235939186856958598">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_737368661">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/737368661" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5238423-nathan" class="left imgcol" title="Nathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1352775557p2/5238423.jpg" alt="Nathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/737368661?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 08, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5238423-nathan" class="user" title="Nathan">Nathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=author-male" class="actionLinkLite">author-male</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=high-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">high-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=rated-4-star" class="actionLinkLite">rated-4-star</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=read-2013" class="actionLinkLite">read-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer737368661">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17400512763021855204"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://fantasyreviewbarn.blogspot.com//2012/11/the-complete-diskworld-re-read.html#more">Complete Discworld Reread</a><br /><br />Pratchett has hit a stride in the Discworld series by now. ‘Interesting Times’ is yet another book where Rincewind runs away from everything he can, the wizards back in the university are hilariously barely competent, we are reminded that the only way to be an old barbarian warrior is to be a very good barbarian warrior.<br /><br />Not a hard hitting satire of anything specific; rather we are treated to a light romp through a revolution in the making, with some of the worst revoluti</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17400512763021855204"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://fantasyreviewbarn.blogspot.com//2012/11/the-complete-diskworld-re-read.html#more">Complete Discworld Reread</a><br /><br />Pratchett has hit a stride in the Discworld series by now. ‘Interesting Times’ is yet another book where Rincewind runs away from everything he can, the wizards back in the university are hilariously barely competent, we are reminded that the only way to be an old barbarian warrior is to be a very good barbarian warrior.<br /><br />Not a hard hitting satire of anything specific; rather we are treated to a light romp through a revolution in the making, with some of the worst revolutionaries in the history of anything. All you need to know about them is their hopes are pinned on Rincewind, the most inept wizard on the Disc. <br /><br />And for once I have literally nothing to say about the book. This reread project is a hell of a lot of fun, but writing reviews for each book is getting harder. What is different from this outing and the last Rincewind book? The setting to a point (faux Asia, and rather crudely done by Pratchett standards), and there is still no shortage of jokes. And it is still fun, real fun. It is a book well worth reading for any fan of the series. But as I said just two short paragraphs ago; Rincewind runs away from everything he can, the wizards back in the university are hilariously barely competent, we are reminded that the only way to be an old barbarian warrior is to be a very good barbarian warrior. <br /><br />So I will waste nobody’s time, let alone my own, and just post a few highlights, which will make zero since to anyone who hasn’t read the book. (Some may argue that posting these highlights is in fact a waste of time, and to them I say this. Nu uh).<br /><br />-The ancient families of Hong, Tang, Fang, Sung, and McSweeney.<br /><br />-Warfare by rumor. Reminding the army that the opponent absolutely does NOT have an army of angry ghosts at their side. Then when evil Lord Hong figures it out and tries to counter the same way, watching it backfire. Sometimes it’s nice to see an evil genius who doesn’t have an answer for everything.<br /><br />-Damn butterflies<br /><br />-Hex is becoming a character, and no one is sure who or what keeps adding on to it.<br /><br />-Learning just what it is Cohan and his Silver Hoard are looking to steal.<br /><br />-Sending the Barking Dog contraption back EXACTLY as they found it. Oh, so good.<br /><br />And some low-lights<br /><br />-Multiple Luggages. I hated this the first time I read the book, still hate it. Takes away everything that makes Luggage unique. Before that he really was a one of a kind force of nature, perhaps the most powerful being in Discworld. Now, meh.<br /><br />-Twoflower showing some spine. It didn’t really fit; he has always been the wide eyed man who sees the best in everything. Looking for revenge didn’t fit at all.<br /><br />‘Interesting Times’ is a perfectly serviceable Discworld book. I just can’t think of anything else to say about it. So I gave up. <br /><br />4 Stars<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17400512763021855204">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_968298200">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/968298200?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 15, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/46008-michele" class="user" title="Michele">Michele</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/46008-michele?shelf=scifi-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">scifi-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer968298200">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1201355434870228608">Other than Hat Full of Sky and the various Witches books in the Discworld series, this is my favorite strictly-in-the-Discworld-series book so far. The Agatean Empire with its enclosing wall, incredibly polite rebels, Red Army and terracotta soldiers is an obvious stand-in for China (and occasionally Japan, with "tsimo" wrestlers of great fatness, kimonos, and samurai). Rather than being or seeming racist or nationalistic, Pratchett is an equal opportunity gadfly regarding human foolishness and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1201355434870228608">Other than Hat Full of Sky and the various Witches books in the Discworld series, this is my favorite strictly-in-the-Discworld-series book so far. The Agatean Empire with its enclosing wall, incredibly polite rebels, Red Army and terracotta soldiers is an obvious stand-in for China (and occasionally Japan, with "tsimo" wrestlers of great fatness, kimonos, and samurai). Rather than being or seeming racist or nationalistic, Pratchett is an equal opportunity gadfly regarding human foolishness and the hunger for power. Yet he is so blessedly funny that the reader who doesn't get (or want to get) any persuasive political statement out of this book will still enjoy it. Cohen the Barbarian gets to be Genghiz Cohen - and what could be better than that?</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1735106550684046670"><strong>Interesting Times</strong> is Terry Pratchett's take on China, which here he calls the Agatean Empire, with its capital at HungHung. At first I was taken aback because much of the action involved my least favorite Diskcworld characters, the undynamic Rincewind and Cohen the Barbarian. (I have always felt that Pratchett is more comfortable with the stews of Ankh-Morpork than in the outlying lands such as Klatch, Lancre, XXXX, Ephebe, and Howandaland.) But fortunately, the story picked up ... because Pratc</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1735106550684046670"><strong>Interesting Times</strong> is Terry Pratchett's take on China, which here he calls the Agatean Empire, with its capital at HungHung. At first I was taken aback because much of the action involved my least favorite Diskcworld characters, the undynamic Rincewind and Cohen the Barbarian. (I have always felt that Pratchett is more comfortable with the stews of Ankh-Morpork than in the outlying lands such as Klatch, Lancre, XXXX, Ephebe, and Howandaland.) But fortunately, the story picked up ... because Pratchett is nothing if not inventive. Look for Lord Hong, the Agatean Grand Vizier, one of the silkiest villains in all of literature (but then Grand Viziers usually are, no?).</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1500896538">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3055374875718174725">Not a bad story, but very simple, predictable, and set in a disappointing world with mostly flat reused characters.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[The author does not create original setting. Everything new was already made in the first books, now we are just revisiting. Anything else is either parody or just straight-up borrowing from existing material. There is way too much missed potential for the sake of dry humor -- an entire continent and culture to explore. How can you make the decision to resort to such</span></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3055374875718174725">Not a bad story, but very simple, predictable, and set in a disappointing world with mostly flat reused characters.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[The author does not create original setting. Everything new was already made in the first books, now we are just revisiting. Anything else is either parody or just straight-up borrowing from existing material. There is way too much missed potential for the sake of dry humor -- an entire continent and culture to explore. How can you make the decision to resort to such unimaginative copying. It just sticks out and overwhelms any good point I would make.<br /><br />The whole Japanese/Chinese parody is borderline racist and just gets tedious after the full line-up of all the age-old stereotypical jokes and historical references. Again, the author has no original setting - the world is just a poorly parodied Far East with a spoonful of pop culture references. It's like the entire author's knowledge about it comes from sitcoms and newspaper anecdotes.<br /><br />I really like Rincewind, even if he gets way too repetitive and predictable. The rest of the characters are as usual - the least interesting ones get the most screen-time, while the ones you'd want to learn more about get pushed into background. The barbarians were a cool concept, but their conversations and jokes got old almost instantly.<br /><br />The writing is fun, but not great. There are some good jokes, but mostly it's one-liners that characters say to each other. Most situations aren't funny because they have stakes and actual real meaning attached. Most things seem to only serve as tools to deliver author's jokes.<br /><br />In the end, it's neither a proper story setting, nor a light comedy. It's trying to be both and failing to be either. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
