    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1467794865">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1467794865" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/43757063-ben-bonci" class="left imgcol" title="Ben Bonci"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Ben Bonci" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1467794865?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 13, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/43757063-ben-bonci" class="user" title="Ben Bonci">Ben Bonci</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times" title="Paperback 9780061056901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1467794865">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4255058410673831090">“Interesting Times” is the end of an ancient curse. It is also the title of Terry Practhett’s 17th Discworld novel. This installment contains all the humor, imagination and drama that defines Pratchett’s seminal fantasy series. But in this case, the book’s particular gimmick is its biggest fault.<br /><br />Interesting Times continues the adventures of Rincewind, the hapless, talentless “wizzard.” That spelling mistake becomes significant when it gets him embroiled in the political affairs of the East-Asia</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4255058410673831090">“Interesting Times” is the end of an ancient curse. It is also the title of Terry Practhett’s 17th Discworld novel. This installment contains all the humor, imagination and drama that defines Pratchett’s seminal fantasy series. But in this case, the book’s particular gimmick is its biggest fault.<br /><br />Interesting Times continues the adventures of Rincewind, the hapless, talentless “wizzard.” That spelling mistake becomes significant when it gets him embroiled in the political affairs of the East-Asia look-alike Agatean Empire.<br /><br />Pratchett handles his complex plot with the ease of expertise. His various subplots, including a group of ancient barbarians learning about civilization, and a romance between two pieces of magical luggage, intermingle with the main plot in various ways. This keeps them distinct, but relevant to the main thrust of the narrative.<br /><br />What really impresses me, though, is Pratchett’s attention to detail. Even exposition gains an extra dimension by the injection of comic subversion, like with the names of the Agatean Empire’s ruling families: Hong, Sung, Tang, McSweeney and Fang. <br /><br />However, part of this brilliantly constructed plot complicates it. The framing device involves a conflict between two gods, Fate, and the Lady (presumed, Luck). What that means for the story of Rincewind, our ill-fated protagonist, is that he doesn’t do too much. Already disposed to run away, now there is a literal deux ex his machina. The B plot is better, but does take a back seat to Rincewind’s escapologist antics. Poor Rincewind doesn’t change, besides getting increasingly cynical about the chances of anything good happening to him. And in that respect, he’s a mediocre protagonist.<br /><br />A book doesn’t need a good protagonist to be good, but it makes it a lot easier. For the case of Interesting Times, the central character was the central issue. Rincewind’s inability to face his own problems made the times a little less interesting.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4255058410673831090">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett">About Terry Pratchett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett"><img alt="1654" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1235562205p2/1654.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer17664747050683931123">Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <em>The Carpet People</em>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17664747050683931123">Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <em>The Carpet People</em>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel, <em>The Color of Magic</em>, in 1983. In 1987, he turned to writing full time. <br /><br />There are over 40 books in the Discworld series, of which four are written for children. The first of these, <em>The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents</em>, won the Carnegie Medal. <br /><br />A non-Discworld book, <em>Good Omens</em>, his 1990 collaboration with Neil Gaiman, has been a longtime bestseller and was reissued in hardcover by William Morrow in early 2006 (it is also available as a mass market paperback - Harper Torch, 2006 - and trade paperback - Harper Paperbacks, 2006). <br /><br />In 2008, Harper Children's published Terry's standalone non-Discworld YA novel, <em>Nation</em>. Terry published <em>Snuff</em> in October 2011. <br /><br />Regarded as one of the most significant contemporary English-language satirists, Pratchett has won numerous literary awards, was named an Officer of the British Empire “for services to literature” in 1998, and has received honorary doctorates from University of Warwick in 1999, the University of Portsmouth in 2001, the University of Bath in 2003, the University of Bristol in 2004, Buckinghamshire New University in 2008, the University of Dublin in 2008, Bradford University in 2009, University of Winchester in 2009, and The Open University in 2013 for his contribution to Public Service.<br /><br />In Dec. of 2007, Pratchett disclosed that he had been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. On 18 Feb, 2009, he was knighted by Queen Elizabeth II.<br /><br />He was awarded the World Fantasy Life Achievement Award in 2010.<br /><br />Sir Terry Pratchett passed away on 12th March 2015.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17664747050683931123">...more</a>

    </div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/22431183-interesting-times">Quotes from Interesting Times</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“Whatever happens, they say afterwards, it must have been fate. People are always a little confused about this, as they are in the case of miracles. When someone is saved from certain death by a strange concatenation of circumstances, they say that's a miracle. But of course if someone is <em>killed</em> by a freak chain of events -- the oil spilled just there, the safety fence broken just there -- that must also be a miracle. Just because it's not nice doesn't mean it's not miraculous.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/122590-whatever-happens-they-say-afterwards-it-must-have-been-fate" class="actionLinkLite">468 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“And therefore education at the University mostly worked by the age-old method of putting a lot of young people in the vicinity of a lot of books and hoping that something would pass from one to the other, while the actual young people put themselves in the vicinity of inns and taverns for exactly the same reason.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/965134-and-therefore-education-at-the-university-mostly-worked-by-the" class="actionLinkLite">19 likes</a>
            </nobr>
